B004K4AUZW|3.0|The Fame and The Fame Monster were truly pop classics.  That said, Gaga's producer on The Fame chose to keep her vocals constrained into more manageable snippets using repetitive phrasing, cool synths and clean dance beats.  With the Fame Monster, the producers decided to let Gaga go into a longer format singing style using more analog instruments and limiting the cut up, simplified and electropop mixes that were on The Fame.<br /><br />With 'Born This Way', the producers have, once again, allowed Gaga to expand her vocals even further choosing to steer away from the cleaner dance pop arrangements into longer format vocals.  Also, they have added electric guitar and other genres into her songs.  While that's not necessarily a bad thing, it does make this album sound much less like the Gaga we have come to know on The Fame.  Instead, this Gaga is actually singing the number (for the most part) rather than singing clips and letting the mix engineer arrange the pieces into a song.<br /><br />That said, I would have preferred a combination somewhere in the middle.  Give us some natural vocal arranged songs like Marry The Night, Born This Way, Judas and Schei&szlig;e, but also give us some lighter dance pop songs like Pokerface, Just Dance and Love Game. Unfortunately, we don't really get much of lighter fare on Born This Way.  So, don't go into this CD expecting much of what you heard on The Fame.  In fact, Born This Way is more like The Fame Monster than The Fame.  In places, it also sounds strikingly like Madonna.<br /><br />On the production side, this CD is way overproduced. There's far too much reverb on many of the songs, the compression is too strong leaving the audio spectrum flat with far too little bottom end.  Comparing production of The Fame to Born This way, there really is no comparison.  The Songs on The Fame are crisp, clear and her vocals are front and center.  On Born This Way, her vocals are buried too deep in the mix, the compression causes the sound to attenuate too much leaving the songs sounding weak and unnatural.  The sound engineer needed to reduce the compression a lot and re-EQ the entire mix to sound clearer and crisper.  Also, whomever decided to go with distorted vocals in this mix should have been pulled from the project.  Distorted vocals never sound good and especially make this CD sound annoying rather than pleasant.<br /><br />As for the songs themselves, I wanted a chance to listen to the songs a bit before reviewing.  Now that I've had a week to digest the compositions and arrangements, here's the rest of this review.  For this CD, Lady Gaga would have done better to hold mix contests.  Put the stems out there and let remixers and DJs have at it. Does that mean the mixes on Born This Way are bad?  Well, let's just say that they are far weaker in arrangements than what's on The Fame.  Extreme care was taken crafting the songs on The Fame and, to some extent, The Fame Monster. Unfortunately, that level of care didn't carry into Born This Way. Now, some of that may be the compositions, but most of it is the choice of beats and choruses.  In some songs, there almost seems to be a case of adult deficit disorder.  That is, lots of mini-songs strung together to create a whole song rather than attempting to craft an entire song.<br /><br />Musically, the most cohesive songs (and the most like The Fame) are Marry The Night, Government Hooker, Judas, Schei&szlig;e, Bloody Mary, Black Jesus + Amen Fashion (which might as well be Madonna) and Born This Way.  Now, that's not to say they sound completely like The Fame, but they do sound the closest to The Fame that you'll find on this release.  The rest of the songs are fair and have their moments, but just aren't cohesive or dare I even say 'filler'.  Specifically, Bad Kids sounds like circa 1979 disco, Fashion Is His Love is just straight mid-80s dance and Electric Chapel just tries way too hard, but never gets there.  In fact, the rest of the songs all try way too hard, but never reach the level of the material on The Fame.  Basically, the melodies the songs not mentioned are too straightforward without a Lady Gaga spin on the material. Literally, anyone could be singing the rest of the material and, worse, it's just not that strong.<br /><br />The second disc is remixes of songs from disc 1. All I have to say about the remixes is, why bother? If these mixes represent the best remixes she could find, I'm at a loss. The best remix on disc 2 is Marry The Night, but unfortunately only lasts for 59 seconds before turning sour.  In fact, for that intro of 59 seconds, that mix sounds amazing and is far better than the 'real' mix on the first disc. I absolutely love the piano only mix for that 59 seconds. it's too bad Lady Gaga (or whomever) didn't choose that sound for the 'real' mix.<br /><br />Finally, the last problem.  I purchased the deluxe edition assuming it would not be censored.  Unfortunately, this is not the Explicit version.  In the Queen track, the arrangers have censored a word from the song leaving a noticeable drop out.  Instead, they could at least get Gaga to sing an alternative word instead of just blanking out the audio.  This sounds cheap and unprofessional.  Be advised if you purchase this edition.. it is not the Explicit version and is clearly edited.material on The Fame. Sad to say, such is the case with GaGa's sophomore recording, \\"Born This Way.\\" While \\"The Fame Monster\\" was a breath of fresh air to a pop/rock field which had become stagnant, \\"Born This Way\\" is stagnant. B001DF68MA|5.0|We purchased this bike seat for our 18 month old after seeing it featured in some celebrity photos in People Magazine. We figured, \\"If it's good enough for Matthew McConaughey, it's good enough for us\\". This bike seat did not disappoint.     The construction and materials are quite superior and very sturdy, yet still very lightweight. The seat comes with padded cushions on the seat, back, and front pedestal which is great for little tushies on long bumpy rides. Our little one actually fell asleep on a ride and was able to use the padded front pedestal as a headrest! The foot rests are vertically adjustable to grow with the child and the seat belts are fully adjustable.    The most notable feature of this bike seat is the forward placement design. Most other seats we looked at were designed for the rear of the bike, which we felt was not ideal since the child is not easily visible nor accessible. With this seat the child is safely tucked in front of the adult rider allowing the rider to easily monitor the child and, if needed, protect the child in the event of a fall. This design also allows the adult rider to safely, quickly, and conveniently mount and dismount the bike without the risk of accidentally kicking the child.     The installation was quite simple (about 1 hour total); however, we did have to remove and re-install the mounting bar twice after a few test rides just to get to perfect fit. I have a mountain bike with an over-sized head tube, but luckily the installation kit comes with extra long hex bolts for this reason. And because the seat is easily removable from the mounting bar with just a single hex bolt, I can still go on solo rides without having to uninstall the whole thing.    The only minor drawbacks (and they are very minor) to this bike seat are that my knees bump into the seat a bit so I end up having to ride with my knees pointed outward; the cushions are not removable for washing, but can be spot cleaned; and lastly, with the mounting bar installed, I cannot raise or lower my own seat without first loosening the mounting bar.    Overall, we absolutely love this bike seat. Our little one loves this seat mostly because he gets a fantastic view of the road as opposed to watching daddy's sweaty back. Because he is safely tucked into my arms during a ride, we can easily talk and hear one another without having to turn around which makes for safer, more interesting and more interactive bike rides.

0596805527|5.0|JavaScript: The Definitive Guide is not just a complete reference of the language, like O'Reilly's other 'thick books,' but also provides a deep dive into JavaScript development. However, if you're just starting out and will be using one of the various libraries (like jQuery), this book may not (yet) be for you.<br /><br />First, the sixth edition is the first I've read, so I can't speak to any changes. Instead, my review is focused on the book as a first-timer reader to the 'series.'<br /><br />JavaScript: The Definitive Guide is broken up into four parts; Core JavaScript, Client-Side JavaScript, the Core JavaScript Reference, and the Client-Side Reference. If you've ever picked up one of O'Reilly's other reference books, like Dynamic HTML: The Definitive Reference, you know about what to expect from those last two parts - a deep reference to the language.<br /><br />The first two parts, however, are a 'deep dive' into the actual language itself. Unlike a mere reference book, JavaScript: The Definitive Guide actually teaches you how to develop in JavaScript, starting at the core fundamentals, and working your way up to more advanced topics.<br /><br />Part of the 'deep dive' aspect also includes following best practices, making numerous references to Douglas Crockford's JavaScript: The Good Parts throughout the first part of the book, which is about 30% of the book. Alone, the first part of the book provides an excellent, near-complete, tutorial on the language.<br /><br />Historical information is also included, which I found to be very interesting when it came up, as well as implementation-specific functionality, that has limited use at this time (and as such, I personally found it distracting, and began skimming over later instances, but it's still nice that it's provided).<br /><br />The second part focuses on the Web aspects, which is quite honestly where most people will be making use of JavaScript. This part covers about what you'd expect, as well as jQuery, client-side storage, and HTML5 functionality.<br /><br />The jQuery information is around 60 pages of content, covers version 1.4, and also includes a bit about jQueryUI (a very little bit). It's quite refreshing to see jQuery included in the book, but as noted initially, if you're looking at focusing just on using a library, it may be better to get a resource focused on just that.<br /><br />The second part is approximately 40% of the book.<br /><br />The third and fourth parts are similar to O'Reilly's other reference books, and are therefore fairly detailed, with examples included. Depending upon your preference, you may find the reference valuable, or prefer searching online. The examples included give the book a slight advantage over the average Web site. Honestly, I generally prefer using online resources, so I don't see myself consulting these later parts very often, if at all.<br /><br />Finally we come to the actual book itself. I received an electronic copy of the book, through the O'Reilly Blogger Review Program, so I can't speak to the quality of a physical copy. However, in the past I have generally found O'Reilly books to be well made, with bindings that last.<br /><br />And now comes the rating.<br /><br />After the first part of this book I was impressed by JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, and could easily have stopped there and been happy. The jQuery inclusion was a nice touch, which may be sufficient to push people who weren't thinking about using a library in their development to doing so, and may actually provide enough information for someone who wants to start learning JavaScript via jQuery. It is, in short, a true guide to JavaScript, and not just a reference book.<br /><br />For these reasons, I must give JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, 5 of 5 stars.The jQuery information is around 60 pages of content, covers version 1.4, and also includes a bit about jQueryUI (a very little bit). It's quite refreshing to see jQuery included in the book, but as noted initially, if you're looking at focusing just on using a library, it may be better to get a resource focused on just that.    The second part is approximately 40% of the book.    The third and fourth parts are similar to O'Reilly's other reference books, and are therefore fairly detailed, with examples included. Depending upon your preference, you may find the reference valuable, or prefer searching online. The examples included give the book a slight advantage over the average Web site. Honestly, I generally prefer using online resources, so I don't see myself consulting these later parts very often, if at all.    Finally we come to the actual book itself. I received an electronic copy of the book, through the O'Reilly Blogger Review Program, so I can't speak to the quality of a physical copy. However, in the past I have generally found O'Reilly books to be well made, with bindings that last.    And now comes the rating.    After the first part of this book I was impressed by JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, and could easily have stopped there and been happy. The jQuery inclusion was a nice touch, which may be sufficient to push people who weren't thinking about using a library in their development to doing so, and may actually provide enough information for someone who wants to start learning JavaScript via jQuery. It is, in short, a true guide to JavaScript, and not just a reference book.    For these reasons, I must give JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, 5 of 5 stars.

My following review is based on the items attributes<br /><br />Ease of Use:<br />It has an on/off button, bass boost switch, and volume control. All in all it is pretty easy to figure out how to use. There is an input label where you plug in from your mp3/cell/whatever into the mini-amp. It has the international headphone symbol where you plug your ears into as well. I would have given it a 5 if not for the volume control, but that will be covered next.<br /><br />Sound Quality:<br />The frequency response was decent and audio is pretty normal. It won't ever make an inferior recording sound good (nothing will) However it is pretty decent at maintaining minimal distortion from the original. Most of the distortion areas typically occur at the cut off areas for MP3 compression so its not really noticeable. But if you are listening to say...a record...or using it as a live-cast headphone amp you'll notice it. I don't know why you would use it for such events though.  With a portable CD player you will get some distortion at the upper and lower frequencies, but for most people, those are about the same frequencies your headphones cut out in. Now, the volume button is pretty much worthless on the box. It won't ever make it any louder than the input line, so its not really a gain control. B0000DZ3EN|2.0|Fans of the dark and dire \\"Return To Oz\\"  (1985) defend it by saying it is closer in style and tone to L. Frank Baum's original Oz books than the cherished MGM \\"The Wizard Of Oz\\" from 1939. I've read quite a few of Baum's Oz books, and I strongly disagree. True, Baum's Oz books do contain more darker elements than the MGM musical. But Baum's books are also philosophical, whimsical, full of humor, happiness, and joy. There is no humor, happiness, or joy in \\"Return To Oz.\\"<br />  I have no objection to a film being closer in style to Baum's books. I don't even mind that this is not a musical. The problem with \\"Return To Oz\\" is its tone and approach. For this, Walter Murch and Gill Dennis, who adapted  Baum's \\"The Marvelous Land Of Oz\\" and \\"Ozma Of Oz\\" into this one film, are entirely to blame. We begin six months after Dorothy (Fairuza Balk) returned home to Kansas from her original Oz adventures. She can't stop talking about Oz, so Aunt Em (Piper Laurie) decides to take Dorothy to a mental hospital for electroshock treatments-- so all of Oz will be erased from her mind!! I assure you, Baum never subjected Dorothy to mental hospitals or electroshock therapy! Surely, Murch and Dennis could have devised another way to return Dorothy to Oz.<br />  As if narrowly escaping electoshock therapy in Kansas wasn't frightening enough, Dorothy soon discovered that Oz is in dis-repair, being taken over by the Wicked Gnome King (Nicol Williamson) and Evil Mombi (Jean Marsh, who played a very similar role, as Evil Queen Bavmorda in \\"Willow\\"; also in 1985). Dorothy makes new friends, Billina, the talking chicken, Tik-Toc, the mechanical man, Pumpkinhead, and something called The Gump-- while discovering that her old Oz friends have all been turned to stone!  And Dorothy's beloved dog Toto has been sadly left behind in Kansas. Meanwhile, Princess Ozma of Oz has been trapped inside of a mirror.  Williamson and Marsh also play the psycho doctor and his stern nurse at the mental hospital in Kansas.<br />  The entire film rests very heavily on the young shoulders of Fairuza Balk as Dorothy, who saves the film from being a complete disaster. Fairuza Balk plays Dorothy with the same wide-eyed innocence that Judy Garland did in 1939. The difference is: Judy had a good script and co-stars to work with. Poor Fairuza is stuck with a terrible script and no co-stars at all to speak of. Fairuza, full of life, is surrounded by a bunch of lifeless animatronic characters from Jim Henson's Creature Shop and too many annoying Claymation effects. Fairuza Balk's performance is all the more extraordinary considering she is trapped in a cinematic disaster and she seems keenly aware of that fact.  You  can't help feeling sorry for poor Fairuza. The film's only other saving grace is the hauntingly beautiful musical score by David Shire.  A justice, of sorts, was done. Fairuza Balk went on to have an interesting adult career, Walter Murch, mercifully, never directed another movie, and \\"Return\\" eventually gained a cult following.  Watching MGM'S 1939 \\"The Wizard Of Oz\\", I always wondered why Dorothy wanted to return to Kansas. Watching this movie, I wonder why anyone would want to watch such a dire version of Oz.  The film takes a preverse delight in its own relentless gloom and doom.  And the relentless darkness sinks this movie.  No, this is not the MGM Oz. But it is not Baum's Oz, either. It is not Oz at all, in fact. It is an odd  Anti-Oz;  the Oz film for \\"Oz fans\\"  who prefer darkness to light, and despair and misery to hope and happiness.Kansas. 0525423273|5.0|Out of all the books I have read in my near-23 years on the planet, I have only cried over three of them.    The first time was when I was 11, and Gavroche's death was too much to bear.    The second time was when I was 19, and Fred Weasley died with the ghost of his last laugh still on his face.    Tonight makes three, and a character I loved didn't even have to die*  It was 2:36 am last Saturday when I finished the book, and I loved it s much that I immediately started scribbling out this review on my sketch pad. I was SUPER stoked to tell you about this book. Once I get my hands on a hardcover (it's currently only on my iPad), I suspect my friends may start to wish I would stop trying to force this book on them. Still, totally worth it. As I was reading I had no idea how it got so late, or where all those crumpled tissues had come from (but to be fair, I also have a cold). But I did know that I had just read what is, in my opinion, the best YA Contemporary romance I had ever read. I don't say that lightly, and I'm not exaggerating at all-- and I don't even generally LIKE contemporary romance, YA or otherwise.    John Green said this book was like if his books had a threesome with Maureen Johnson's and Sarah Dessen's books and then had a baby. I was expecting that to mean it would be a little like all three. No no, my friend. Stephanie Perkins takes all of the best elements of each to the fullest.** For example, in one scene Anna draws a chocolate mustache on herself in order to cheer up St. Clair, and it reads just like an MJ tweet.    But in my opinion, Perkins also excels in her own right when it comes to the execution of the love story. It starts slow and fairly shallow, but the relationship between Anna and St. Clair grows slowly and becomes more about the same elements that make for a really good friendship-- trust, empathy, and support. In the beginning we are bombarded by descriptions of how hot St. Clair is. I was worried we had a Cullen situation on ourhands. But just like Anna, the reader falls in love with St. Clair's personality, and by the middle of the book St. Clair looked goofier and shorter in my mind, his temper and other faults got a smidge frustrating, and he was just an everyday, real-life guy. By the time you hit the chapter that is comprised of emails between Anna and St. Clair, you'll love him too... but you'll still totally call him on his b.s., should it arise.    I also loved how Anna is complex in that John Green kind of way, where she is the protagonist because she is trying to solve the Big Problem she has with her self. Still, Perkins manages to make that core idea completely her own. Unlike Green's novels where the protagonists have generally already identified what they perceive to be their Big Problem, Anna has to work out 1) that she has an internal problem and 2) what that problem could be.      Lastly... Paris. Wow. I hadn't really had a desire to go to Paris prior to reading this (London and Cork are way higher on the list), but now I will have to rethink that. Paris is essentially a character in the book as well, and Perkins incorporates its details in an incredibly fluid manner. This is even more impressive after you've visited her website, where says that the only really significant time she spent in Paris was the month of January, 2010. She rented an apartment and apparently memorized the nuances of the city.   I'm going to have to read this book many, many more times. The trick is going to be getting myself to read it primarily to watch the execution of the storyline at least once without getting swept away by the love story. I don't know if I can do it.      *I suppose that's technically a spoiler, but you can tell from all the indirect Nicholas Sparks jabs in the first 20 pages that this is not going to be the kind of love story where the starcrossed protagonists fall in love and one of them dies a tragic, cancery death.   **But I haven't read Sarah Dessen yet (shame! Shame on me!), so I can't vouch for John's claim fully
0060933275|5.0|In 1957, Harper Lee took her manuscript entitled, Atticus, a story about a southern lawyer who defends an innocent black man accused of rape, to various New York publishers only to be met with rejection after rejection.<br /><br />Three years later after many rewrites, the book retitled, Too Kill a Mockingbird was published to wide acclaim. One of the endearing elements in this work is the marvelous description of a small town in the 1930s during the depression and its people who are viewed through a magnifying glass. Throughout the book, there is a consciousness of how one behaved in a small town back then and the way one behaved was central to who one was.<br /><br />In short, the story is pure truth and the impact of the raw language used as in the case of the \\"N\\" word which was an acceptable way of speaking in those days might give offense to today's readers.<br /><br />The story is told through the eyes of a child, nicknamed Scout who serves as narrator but as an adult looking back. Scout is a girl and a tomboy who speaks with her fists and is curious about the world around her. Her father named Atticus Finch, whom she addresses by his first name, is a soft spoken very dignified attorney who has earned the respect of the town not only by his reputation but also for being kindhearted when he accepts payment from clients in kind as he understands they cannot afford to pay him in cash.<br /><br />The story's structure centers on the town courthouse where Atticus defends Tom Robinson, a black work hand who has been accused of rape by the daughter of his employer. The story is both dramatic and powerful when one considers the courage is took to write what is was like to be in a black person's skin from the point of view of a white person. And the fact that it was published at the beginning of the civil rights movement. There is an understanding of the nature of the harshness of humankind exemplified through legalized segregation which is an amplification of the south in the 1930s without being judgmental about it. We know the injustice that is being created against Tom Robinson as there is no proof that he committed a crime but only the prejudices of the townsfolk that formulate the opinion that he is guilty and seal his fate.<br /><br />There are many characters is this classic story. One in particular is Boo Radley, Scout's next door neighbor whom she has never seen and through invented tales by her friends and neighbors she comes to view Boo as a menacing phantom capable of murder. It is the spreading of rumors about people and the harm it can cause that is also expressed in the story, which unfolds dramatically and brilliantly with Tom Robinson, Atticus, Scout and Boo interwoven.<br /><br />Charles Blanchard<br />author of Mourning Doves After The Firetal about it. 0375842209|5.0|The Book Thief is a powerful historical fiction novel, set in Nazi Germany.  Although the story takes place in the historical era of the Second World War and the Holocaust, it is not merely a factual retelling of history. It is geared toward young adults, as it explores the thoughts and emotions of a young German girl who, in this setting, progresses through adolescence and experiences many pivotal changes in her life.                                                                                                            While the story focuses mainly on the girl, Liesel Meminger, the narrator is actually Death.  He tells how he took Liesel's brother, Werner, when his mother was bringing him and Liesel to live in Molching with foster parents Hans and Rosa Hubermann.  Liesel is continuously haunted by his memory and begins stealing books, perhaps attempting to compensate for loss.  In her new home, she learns to read with the help of Hans, her beloved stepfather, and Max Vandenburg, a Jew in hiding.  She learns the power of words and ultimately uses them to create her own story- one populated with unforgettable characters like Ilsa Hermann, the woman who inspires this, and Rudy Steiner, her neighbor and best friend.    Markus Zusak employs a desperately hopeful tone throughout the novel.  The plot is built around characters in dismal situations.  While these characters find themselves in temporary despair, courage usually saves them in the end.  Frau Holtzapfel is one such character.  She experienced a tremendous amount of despair since one of her sons was killed at Stalingrad, and the other committed suicide shortly after he returned.  At first, she was tempted to take her own life, but Liesel's words gave her the strength to carry on.  The mood the reader experiences is definitely dark, but with a beautiful glimmer of hope.    Zusak strives to bring out the beauty and brutality of humans in Nazi Germany, something by which I was intrigued as a reader.  People have a tendency to vilify the Germans, presuming that they were all Nazis and violent persecutors of Jews.  The Book Thief contradicts such beliefs by presenting characters like Hans, who are opposed to Hitler's ideas and feel compassion for the persecuted.  Zusak does an excellent job, not only with historical accuracy, but also poignant portrayal of human feelings in a time of war and Death.  My only criticism is the lack of clarity regarding whether Liesel's mother survived.  I would have liked confirmation on this matter.

B0050N8N0Q|5.0|Foster the People burst on to the alternative music scene when their first single, Pumped Up Kicks, began receiving heavy airplay in the latter months of 2010.  The track details an eerie account of a latchkey kid, whose distorted reality leads him to wield a weapon and deliver the ominous warning, \\"better run, better run, faster than my bullet.\\"  The song's upbeat and cavalier style, which bears a striking contrast to the sinister lyrical content, piqued the interest of listeners and earned Foster the People instant notoriety.  However, their EP album was simply a tease, and it left their newfound devotees clamouring for more.  The release of their first full length album, Torches, is the antidote to the feverish pleadings of their trip hop-craved following.<br /><br />The group's global sound alludes to something like a fusion of MGMT and Passion Pit's signature electronica stylings, with a hint of Two Door Cinema Club thrown in the mix.  The heavy hooks and infectious rhythms are practically begging to be absorbed with the car windows rolled down and the volume jacked up, whilst head bobbing and steering wheel tapping ensue.  A combination of reverb-heavy synths and sleek instrumentation, in addition to the laconic delivery of alluring, intentionally repetitive lyrics, create dance-worthy tracks that are both accessible and instantly endearing.<br /><br />Despite the fact that it is a debut album, Torches is certain to leave Foster the People deeply entrenched in the alternative landscape.  Not all of the songs contain the magical elements that garnered the attention of  \\"Pumped up Kicks,\\" but they do coalesce to form a uniquely satisfying auditory experience.  Furthermore, although there is a notable lack of diversity amongst tracks, they have established a formula that doesn't eagerly invite tampering.  This is certainly a group worth following, as their raw potential is largely uncharted in an industry that seems preoccupied with providing easily digestible, ubiquitous pop to the masses.masses.

Sure, it's a farce, it's sexist, and it's not politically correct, but it's also a lot of fun.  Wayne pretty much plays himself in this comedy, co-starring the fiesty and lovely Maureen O'Hara.  This was the fourth pairing of the two stars, (previously they appeared together in Rio Grande, The Quiet Man, The Wings of Eagles), and their on-screen chemistry is real and intense.  The two carry this movie on their collective shoulders without slouching.  Their on-going relationship battle is the framework from which this movie moves forward, and is akin to the chess game that Wayne plays early on in the film: one makes a move, and the other reacts (usually in a very funny way). It is not meant to be a serious film whatsoever; it was made for fun, a chance to pair Wayne with Maureen again, and with Wayne being film money magnet, a chance for the production company to turn a nifty profit.<br /><br />It has been written that at some point in John Wayne's career, he started to become the characters he played on the big screen - life imitating art.  Perhaps this film begins the transformation process.  Through the character of McClintok, Wayne dispenses his views on what it means to be a man, what a man-woman relationship should be, the role of government in life, his view of politicians, freedom, self-expression, and even race relations.  O'Hara, every bit Wayne's equal in this film, shows her natural fiestiness, and what every woman wants in her man - someone who will pursue her with passion, manliness, and romance.<br /><br />The supporting cast is a pretty good one, with Yvonne D'Carlo, Patrick Wayne, Steffanie Powers, Chill Wills, Jerry Van Dyke (Dick's brother), and Edgar Buchanan (Uncle Joe of Petticoat Junction fame).<br /><br />The film is noted for its unforgetable and oh so fun mud fight, and the grande finale, when Wayne chases down O'Hara on main street at high noon, in order to give her a public spanking, and winning her back to his side in the process.  During the film, O'Hara's character, Katie, says she wants a cultured, sensitive man, yet, what she really wants is Wayne to show her in a demonstrative way that he loves her, is devoted to her, and will still romance her.  Fittingly, the showdown on main street, has Wayne doing just that, although in his unique manly way.<br /><br />There's nothing profound here, no dramatic acting or academy awards, just a fun film with two stars that shine like bright headlights.<br /><br />koneon main street at high noon, in order to give her a public spanking, and winning her back to his side in the process. She misses things, she makes assumptions that someone with more years wouldn't make, she suffers a great deal of conflict where these two guys are concerned. You can't help but root for her, and for them, even though she can't end up with both of them. You feel her attraction to Gale, even if she denies that there's anything romantic between them.  You feel her conflict about Peeta, the boy with the bread. I admit, though, that I love a good love triangle!  This is a good love triangle.  Both Peeta and Gale are desirable, in different ways, and in her feelings about them, the reader is almost as torn as Katniss herself.<br /><br />The Story:<br /><br />Okay, this is where the disturbing comes in. This story is dark. It's not for the faint of heart. It's about children (ages 12-18) killing one another. They're doing it for survival, and for the entertainment of the people in the Capitol of Panem. This is not a feel good, happy-go-lucky book. It's a live or die book. There are some reviewers on Goodreads who took strenuous objection to the whole kids killing kids thing, and while it's very poignant, I agree that it's difficult to stomach. What I don't agree with is that this book should be skipped. This book is great, the setting is dark, but that's what makes it good literature. It has a point. The setting is very well thought out, the actions and reactions are very clear and often unsettling. You see why they're doing it, how it's received in the Districts, and the difference between the people dying to survive in the Districts from those in the Capitol, where there's wealth. This is dystopian and dystopian is not always pretty. As a matter of fact, it's never pretty. That's what makes dystopian what it is. It always has a point. Always. I suppose it comes down to this: If you can't stomach kids killing kids, don't read it. Otherwise, try.<br /><br />The Verdict:<br /><br />I loved it and recommend it to anyone.  Not just anyone who enjoys dystopian, but to everyone. I don't generally read dystopian and I loved this book. If you've been on the fence, and think you can deal with the unsettling themes, go get it.  You won't regret it.n the fence, and think you can deal with the unsettling themes, go get it.  You won't regret it.

B004V9J3HM|5.0|After several spins, I'd have to say that this album ends up being a strong effort that links his earlier musical styles from his Snow/Testimony era to his latest styles found in his Whirlwind/Lifeline era quite adeptly, though I'd certainly rate Testimony 2 as quite a bit stronger than the very uneven Lifeline. While this album does a nice job of incorporating some of the musical themes of Testimony to really make this new album feel like a very natural continuation of the first Testimony album, he also definitely goes quite a ways beyond that musical template to explore some nice progressive styles that end up being very enjoyable and pretty fresh sounding. In fact, I'd say this is a more \\"progressive\\" sounding effort than either Testimony 1 or Snow.<br /><br />There are the traditional Nealesque type of themes and overtures you'd expect from most of his albums, but for the most part, they are different enough to not sound stale or self-derivative. That being said, there are a few parts of the album are very much \\"been there, done that\\", like the Gentle Giant-type vocal piece in the track \\"Time Changer\\", however in that particular instance I get the sense that it's more an homage to that era of his career with Spock's Beard than a guy running out of new ideas. It's not a \\"perfect\\" album like \\"Bridge Across Forever\\" or \\"The Light\\" and there are a couple of tracks like Jayda that aren't quite on par with the rest of the album, but this is one of Neal's best solo efforts since the One/? albums.<br /><br />I'd rank this album on par with Snow, Testimony and One. B004UFEJS0|5.0|Pixar and director Brad Bird hits another one out of the park with an entertaining, funny and even informative take on haute cuisine. Ratatouille combines themes such as a rags to riches story, father and son dynamics, living a double life, and of course rats and food. Pixar has always been good with smart family entertainment and this movie is no different. My four year old son loves it. My 20 year old daughter loves it. I love it. This is one of those films that will undoubtedly endure the test of time.     The basic story is a rat with a keen sense of smell and a love for making food gets separated from his family and ends up at the restaurant of his favorite chef. In a strange turn of events Remy (the rat voiced by Patton Oswalt) creates a soup that everybody loves, but credit was given to the garbage boy. Remy and said garbage boy Linguini (Lou Romano) come up with a plan to keep Remy cooking with Linguini as the cover. From there you get plenty of hijinks, cartoon thrills, a little romance, some character development, and all around fun times.     It doesn't matter how good the animation is on a movie. If the script doesn't deliver the movie won't either (just look at Final Fantasy). Ratatouille's is sharp. It hits all the marks in story development and gives a really nice pacing on events. My only gripe is there was an action scene or two that felt forced just to add some excitement while not really lending to the story very well. Other than that the plot was pitch perfect.     While a great story is the backbone to a good movie good animation doesn't hurt. I can't really say much that most people who watch Pixar movies already know. The animation excels. The fine details from the hairs on the rats and humans to the look of the food to the stylistic backgrounds. It all shows quality. I can't help think a bit of The Incredibles theory is in the design, which would make sense considering Brad Bird directed that one too.      Another thing that can make or breakthe immersion level of an animated movie is the sound and voice acting. Pixar always finds a way to get the best out of their actors and make them sound like professional voice talents. It's all great, but I would like to make note of my personal standouts. Lou Romano as Linguini gives a ton of character for somebody who doesn't have much of a voice acting background (he's a design artist). I didn't recognize comedian Janeane Garofalo as Colette she was so convincing. More so for Ian Holm as the main villain Skinner. He knocked it out of the park for me with that outlandish character. Then you have Peter O'Toole as food critic Anton Ego. This man steals the show with his voice and the subtle intonation in his performance resonates.    This Blu-Ray is a re-release of the 2007 Blu-Ray with an additional DVD version added. The special features are identical to the previous release. Here's what you get(* denotes feature on the DVD as well):    Pixar Shorts*:    Lifted - One of my favorite shorts about an alien abduction... final exam. The human character looks a lot like our human hero Linguini from the movie.     Your Friend the Rat - This is actually a very educational and informative short on the history of rats and their relationship to human society. It's told by Remy and his brother and is very entertaining.     Fine Food & Film* - Interesting featurette with Brad Bird and top chef Thomas Keller on the food designed for the movie.     Cine-Explore - Tons of mini documentaries and interviews can be picked up while watching the movie. Just about everything you wanted to know. I believe you can get this stuff on the main menu as well without using Cine-Explore.     Gusteau's Gourmet Game - Never played it. Supposed to be Linguini making food during an increasingly busy meal rush.     Deleted Scenes* - Three of them, and they are not finished beyond rough animation. Interesting stuff, but nothing you would have missed.     Deleted Scenes R.I.P. - More deleted scenes.I haven't seen them, but I heard they are better than the previous deleted scenes.     I am assuming the Easter Eggs are also still in here. Wait for the rat to appear on the main menu and then hit the left directional key. You get five bonus items on an hidden menu.     Ratatouille is an amazingly entertaining film and this release has a lot of great extras on top of a great movie. If you have one of those households that has both Blu-Ray players and DVD players (maybe in the kids room) this is a good buy as it keeps both version inside one case. If you are a BD or DVD only household it may be better for you to go for the 2007 release if it's cheaper.

B004K4AVAG|2.0|Heard of Lady Gaga? She's this loopy pop singer with weird hair and clothes, and most people either love her or hate her. Well, my personal opinion of Lady Gaga's persona won't come into this -- it's all about her new album, \\"Born This Way.\\" Well, brace yourself: It's a bloated mess that rambles on far too long, with too many songs, too much noise, and no unifying style.<br /><br />\\"Born This Way\\" presents itself as a gay anthem, and it does this by thumping you over the head with obvious shout-outs like \\"Don't be a drag/just be a queen\\" and \\"No matter gay, straight or bi/Lesbian, transgendered life.\\" But all Madonna comparisons aside, this song is a mess -- the producers heaped so many layers of sound that it sometimes sounds like Gaga is being smothered.<br /><br />Then there's \\"Judas,\\" the second single of this album. This suffers from the same choking overproduction as \\"Born This Way,\\" with Gaga's voice being smushed down under a thick fog of synthpop. Then again, she spends half the song squawking, \\"Juda-JUDA-AW!\\" like an epileptic goose, so that might be a good thing.<br /><br />But what of the other songs? Well, the album actually starts quite strong with the dark, glittering \\"Marry the Night,\\" and the bleakly desperate sex-with-presidents thumper \\"Government Hooker.\\" But things start going off the rails with the goopy romanticism of the Spanish-flavored \\"Americano,\\" the vaguely Germanic club melody \\"Scheibe,\\" and the cheesy \\"Hair.\\"<br /><br />After that, Gaga just... seems to stop trying, and lets the following songs decay into a soggy mess of mediocre club pop. Some of the songs barely have melodies (\\"Black Jesus - Amen Fashion,\\" \\"Highway Unicorn (Road to Love)\\"), and some are just so bland that it's hard to remember them (\\"The Queen\\"). I like \\"Electric Chapel,\\" but most of these songs could have been cut without anyone noticing.<br /><br />She does rouse herself enough to give distinctive sounds to the last couple songs... but they're AWFUL sounds: \\"You And I\\" sounds like a country singer wandered into a dance club and just started singing, and \\"The Edge of Glory\\" is a horrendously cheesy power ballad, complete with SAXOPHONES.<br /><br />In a nutshell, \\"Born This Way\\" is a huge bloated mess, bogged down with excess and collapsing under its own weight. It feels like Lady Gaga is trying to do EVERYTHING in this album. More sound! More musical genres! More random references to fashion, nightclubs, sex and gay people! More dance songs that nobody can remember when they're over!<br /><br />As for Gaga's lyrics, they have some interesting elements, like the strong undercurrent of Catholic/religious symbolism and the idea that \\"bad kids\\" are just fine because they were \\"born that way.\\" But her writing also tends to be insanely clumsy (\\"You're black, white, beige, chola descent/You're Lebanese, you're Orient\\"), and her symbolism is so obvious that it leaves bruises between your eyes.<br /><br />And her voice? It's the strongest part of any song where she tries, but too often it falls by the wayside in this album. In some songs, it's swamped by all those electronic layers, and in some songs she just sort of croons without putting any effort into it.<br /><br />If they had trimmed off the B-sides and the extra production, \\"Born This Way\\" could have been a pretty decent pop album. But instead, it feels like a collapsing monument to pop excess.red into a dance club and just started singing, and \\"The Edge of Glory\\" is a horrendously cheesy power ballad, complete with SAXOPHONES. For example:<br />On page 28 you are told, \\"On a clear day...the shadows are darker and bluer relative to the sun. As more clouds appear in the sky, shadows become grayer.\\"<br />Then, \\"The blue sky color gives the top of the shoulder a greenish quality in the shadow. Where the sleeve drops from the shoulder, the yellow shirt picks up warm colors from the ground and appears more orange.\\"<br />Going with the past section of how sunny days have blue shadows, it's like, 'Oh, that makes sense!'<br />Then my mind immediately jumps to, 'Well, if the shadows are greenish on a yellow shirt, they'd be more violet on a red shirt, and possibly more lilac on a cloudy day.'<br />I highly recommend Colors and Light, and it's so affordable, too! There's also a second book by this author, [[ASIN:0740785508 Imaginative Realism: How to Paint What Doesn't Exist]].<br />As for the book itself, it's made really well, with heavy gloss paper that's not too shiny, and very durable. Oh, but don't leave it in a really hot car or place, as this type of paper/material tends to warp if left in such conditions for too long.

B004NCLKJG|5.0|None of the previous reviewers has offered much information to supplement their gushing praise for this broadcast of Die Walkure from Feb. 24, 1968. In small print the booklet informs us that this was part of Herbert von Karajan's ambitious but ill-fated Ring at the Met, which accounts for the presence of great voices in every part. Critics and audiences objected to Karajan's dimly lit, abstract staging, and various conflicts (including a moment in rehearsal when Birgit Nilsson broke a strand of pearls in her frustration with the conductor -- if I remember right) led to its termination of the conductor's participation after the staging of Rheingold and Walkure.<br /><br />But in hindsight one can see what a gigantic leap forward this production represented for the Met, then saddled with routine Wagner conductors (at best), musty staging, and a scrappy orchestra. The Levine era was just around the corner; still, Karajan brought international standards to the pit. there is a broadcast of Walkure from 1969 where he actually conducts. Here we get a serviceable substitute in the Croatian conductor Berislav Klobucar, then in his mid-Forties; he was conducting at Bayreuth during this period, also, but his leadership cannot be said to substitute for Karajan, even though orchestral execution is more secure than the norm for that era at the Met.<br /><br />The sound is goodish mono, with the voices caught close up and realistically. The orchestra isn't too recessive, but they are secondary in the engineering and sound rather dry -- don't expect sumptuousness.  As for the singers, Vickers and Stewart reprise Siegmund and Wotan from Karajan's contemporary Ring cycle on DG; as Brunnhilde and Fricka, Nilsson and Ludwig arrive from Ring cycles under Solti and Bohm. At the time Hans Hotter was nearing the end of his career as a vocally viable Wotan, the other candidate at the time being Theo Adam. As a fan of Stewart's young-sounding, fierce Wotan, I'm grateful that he steps in -- for the 1969 broadcast the Wotan was a more pedestrian Adam, whose reputation in Wagner baffles me. Leonie Rysanek had sung Sieglinde under many conductors, . The Hunding is Karl Ridderbusch, a Karajan favorite who went on to sing Sachs under him; here he is vocally impressive but rather lacking in ferocity. By any standard this is a cast for the ages, and it satisfies old hands who regretted at the time that Karajan and Solti had divvied up the greatest Bayreuth veterans, leaving both their recorded Ring cycles sounding incomplete (but with Solti getting the best of the lot, all told).<br /><br />In the end, if you don't mind monaural sound and Klobucar's fairly ordinary conducting, this historical Walkure is self-recommending and augurs well for all the Met broadcasts that Sony has been allowed to release, after decades when the Met zealously guarded its recording rights in the U.S.ast the Wotan was a more pedestrian Adam, whose reputation in Wagner baffles me. B004KWVDWI|4.0|Papillon (drama, biography, crime)<br />Directed by Franklin J. Schaffner<br />Starring Steve McQueen and Dustin Hoffman<br /><br />Warner Bros.   1973   151 min   Rated PG   Released May 24, 2011<br /><br />Video:<br />Video codec: MPEG-4 AVC<br />Video resolution: 1080p<br />Aspect ratio: 2.40:1<br /><br />Audio:<br />English: DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1<br /><br />Subtitles:<br />English SDH, French, Spanish<br /><br />Disc:<br />Single 50GB Blu-ray Disc<br /><br />The Film 4/5<br /><br />Papillon is a film I hadn't seen for approximately 30 years, so I was eagerly awaiting this Blu-ray release. I was a different person back then and my tastes have evolved a great deal since first seeing the film. What was a masterpiece in my memory is now simply a good film.<br /><br />The story is set in a prison in French Guiana and is based on the true story of Henri Charriere. He was known as Papillon because of the butterfly tattooed on his chest. Steve McQueen plays the role and does an excellent job.<br /><br />The other key character is Louis Dega (Hoffman), who is a forger and not well-equipped to fend for himself in prison. He hires Papillon for protection and promises to finance Papillon's escape attempt. It proves to be a wise decision.<br /><br />On reaching the prison, the new inmates are given a welcome speech. They are told that their first escape attempt would result in two years of solitary confinement being added to their sentence. A second attempt would mean five more years in solitary. Extreme cases would result in death by guillotine.<br /><br />Nearby islands contain prison work camps and Dega and Papillon are sent there. The swampy conditions are full of natural dangers such as crocodiles and snakes. Any escapees are hunted by former prisoners for a reward. Papillon makes an early attempt and is quickly recaptured, resulting in the promised two years in solitary confinement.<br /><br />The rations in solitary are barely enough to support human life and Dega bribes guards to smuggle Papillon extra food. When this is discovered, the warden wants the name of the person sending him food. Papillon refuses and is put on half rations. This is equivalent to a death sentence, but Papillon eats whatever creatures stray into his cell and manages to survive the two-year ordeal.<br /><br />When he recovers his strength, Papillon makes another escape attempt. Dega and another prisoner join him and they arrange to leave the swamp by boat. The three set sail for Honduras. On reaching land they eventually become separated and Papillon is forced to fend for himself. This leads to an extended sequence, largely without dialogue, where Papillon lives with some of the indigenous population. It's a happy time for him and is a stark contrast to his life in prison.<br /><br />I won't reveal any more of the story, but there are a few more twists and turns before it ends.<br /><br />The story is essentially about the strength of the human spirit and the desire to be free. Papillon has to endure harsh conditions, but something inside him makes him go on. It also shows that some criminals are not all bad. The friendship between Papillon and Dega is powerful and lasting. The final sequence shows both the friendship and Papillon's determination to live as a free man.<br /><br />Video Quality 3.5/5<br />The opening scene contains heavy grain and looks terrible, but isn't representative of the remainder of the film. It soon settles down and delivers a fairly pleasing transfer. The film is nearly 40 years old and doesn't look like a recent release, but the Blu-ray offers a big upgrade over previous releases. Just take a look at the trailer to see how the original film must have looked. Although the detail isn't as strong as I would have liked, it's adequate. Brighter outdoor scenes benefit the most. Colors are strong and accurate and the print looks fairly clean.<br /><br />Audio Quality 4/5<br />An early conversation between Papillon and Dega aboard the prison ship is problematic. The dialogue appears rather distant and there are minor synchronization issues. This is an isolated incident and the quality quickly improves. The DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 mix does a good job of adding to the brooding atmosphere of the film. Heavy rain sounds like it's supposed to and jungle sounds come across in the rear speakers. The Oscar-nominated score sounds impressive and it's a good presentation overall.<br /><br />Special Features 2/5<br /><br />Aside from a trailer, there's only one additional feature:<br /><br />The Magnificent Rebel (12:19) is brief and shot in SD, but explains some of the problems involved in shooting the film. Some of the locations were hard to reach and had never been seen on camera before. There's also a sequence showing the reaction of the real Papillon, Henri Charriere, visiting the set and talking about how authentic it was.<br /><br />34-page digibook<br /><br />Papillon is a long film at 151 minutes, and some of it isn't pleasant to watch. It's not supposed to be. If you have seen Midnight Express, you'll have some idea of what to expect. The story takes its time to develop, but the payoff is worth it. McQueen may have been chosen because of his character in The Great Escape, but he has to show more range here and pulls it off well. Hoffman is familiar as the nervous Dega and the friendship is believable. While imperfect, the Blu-ray presentation offers a considerable upgrade and the digibook looks great. Fans of the film shouldn't hesitate to pick it up.<br /><br />Overall score 4/5DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 mix does a good job of adding to the brooding atmosphere of the film. 1401923127|2.0|As happens with virtually every New Age writer that I've read (and every New Age believer I've encountered), Bruce Lipton presents a confusing and hard to pin down philosophy that seems to obscure any attempt to rationally evaluate what is being said.  As you read, you find yourself begging silently for some clarification of how he is defining terms, and what it is that he's really trying to advocate.   An example would be his use of the word `behavior.'  He claims that behavior is determined by environment, but this conclusion comes following an elaborate (and nicely written) description of how cells test and respond to their environment.  But what he is describing is the mechanics of animation within the cell the automatic responses to the environment of the various cellular proteins.  This is not even \\"behavior\\" on the cellular level.  It certainly can't be expanded to imply anything meaningful for a staggeringly-complex organism like the human being.  Behavior is chosen by a sentient being.  It is not stimulus-response, it is thought-choice.   Another would be the way he defines `spirit.'  He says, \\"We are all imbued with a spirit, the invisible energy.\\"  This comes after a long (and again, well written) discussion of basic quantum physics, which tell us that all matter, at the quantum level, is really energy.  The argument goes that since matter is energy and we are made of matter, we are energy.  That's no problem so far, but suddenly in the discussion this energy is renamed spirit.  So because all matter is energy, all matter is imbued with a spirit.  God is in everything, everything is God.   This category confusion permeates Lipton's book.  In one place he claims that since cell receptors can respond to resonant energy, such as light, sound and radio waves, \\"...biological behavior can be controlled by invisible forces, including thought...\\"  Here he is implying (I think) that we can will ourselves to better health.  This is flawed logic.  In essence, he isbuilding this pair of syllogisms:    Syllogism One:  Light, sound and radio wave energy have been shown to affect cell behavior.  Wave energy is invisible.  Therefore:  invisible forces can affect cell behavior.    No problem so far.  This is both valid and true.    Syllogism Two:  Invisible forces can affect cell behavior (taken from the conclusion above).  Thought is an invisible force.  Therefore:  thought can affect cell behavior.    It's not hard to see the logical error here when you lay it out this way.  The second premise is unsubstantiated and debatable (whether or not thought is a force).  But more importantly, the reasoning is fallacious.  It commits the fallacy of hasty generalization by assuming that all invisible forces, including thought, can affect cell behavior.  This is not at all established in his reasoning.     This is the kind of thing that permeates Lipton's book.  In many of his claims, he assumes the validity of Darwinian evolution and then uses it to explain his claims.  This is classically circular.   Ultimately, Lipton launches into a dissertation on human-caused global climate change, claiming that we are killing our species by changing the environment with which we are so finely attuned.  Again here, Lipton assumes the truth of his claim in order to support his claim.  So again he begs the question.   In places, Lipton's facts are somewhat iffy, which makes one wonder whether he can be trusted on matters strictly related to biology.  For instance, he laments the treatment of Jean-Baptiste Lamark, who Lipton claims was abused because he understood the effect of the environment on cells.  As far as I know, and a quick Wikipedia search will verify this, Lamark was ridiculed for his belief that traits acquired in an organism's lifetime could be passed on to offspring.  For instance, he supposed that the Giraffe's neck became long because over time, Giraffes stretched and strained their necks to reach higher branches.  This would create minute changes in the length of their necks during their lifetimes, and that these traits were heritable.  So although Lamark seems to have had a good understanding that environment effects gene expression and other cell behavior, he was not ostracized for that belief, as Lipton claims.   Finally, I question that Mr. Lipton's great epiphany; the insight that the cell nucleus and its DNA are not the \\"brain\\" of the cell, is the breakthrough that he claims it to be.  I don't recall any scientist making the claim that DNA \\"decides\\" anything for the cell.  It has always been thought of as the blueprint for cellular activities.  The fact that Lipton thinks of the cell membrane as the \\"brain\\" of the cell is very instructive and an interesting way to look at it, but I don't see how it is quite the amazing insight he seems to believe it is.   Lipton is quite masterful at explaining the complicated workings of the cell so that lay readers can understand and appreciate the wonders and complexity of our bodies.  That is where his talent lies, and I would enjoy reading a book by Mr. Lipton that sticks to shedding light on the intricacies of biological life.  But when he ventures into mysticism based on the mere fact that matter is energy and energy resonates, so obviously everything has a spirit, I have to look elsewhere for insight.

0441018866|1.0|(recommendations on what to read instead of this book at the bottom of my review)    This book doesn't qualify as science fiction.  Science fiction introduces a technological/scientific development and imagines what would happen to human beings as a result.  This book is a fantasy whodunnit - not a really good one - that uses stolen scifi concepts as a backdrop.     The story starts off using Wil McCarthy's concept from The Collapsium, of \\"planettes\\": asteroids with a semisafe black hole core, giving them earthlike gravity.  Not that Reynolds makes much use of it - he just throws it out there as a by-the-by sort of thing and moves on with... whatever it was he thought he was doing.    The story moves on to copying practically every concept from Vernor Vinge's Qeng Ho novels:  A widespread galactic civilization knit together by slow-moving traders who use suspended animation while in flight, living essentially on a different time scale than people living on planets, organized into \\"trading families\\" that synchronize reunions every few thousand years or so.    Having chosen some excellent scifi concepts to work with, Reynolds essentially ignores them.  The litmus test for a story claiming to be scifi is - if you replace the spaceships with horses, can you make a western out of this book?  Yes you can, and that's how you can tell it isn't true science fiction - with a real scifi novel, the science or tech is integral - remove the science fiction element from it and it doesn't work.      The ships in House of Suns ARE used just like horses in a western.  Near the end, there is a fast pursuit chase that lasts for THOUSANDS of years - the pursued and the pursuers both have to do the thing in suspended animation - and there isn't a single mention of how the ships keep going for longer than the Roman Empire existed with no refueling, repairs, overhaul, etc.  For an adventure story, that's fine - you don't worry all that much about making sure the horse is fed, sheltered, orthe finer points of hoof care in a western.  But in a scifi novel, how things work does matter.    There are even specific scenes within the novel that seem \\"lifted\\" - at one point they are in a ship dodging a powerful but slow beam weapon.  It reads VERY much like a scene straight out of Wil McCarthy's Collapsium in which the main character is attempting to avoid a nasen beam.  It's hard to tell the full extent of the \\"lifting\\" of materials because things keep occurring to me... those things I don't recognize are probably from books I haven't read yet or things in them I've forgotten.  For instance there's a scene in which a bad guy's ship is blown up... and a smaller, scary/evil/cool looking ship emerges from the wreckage.  That seemed so familiar to me when I read it but I couldn't place it - and it just occurred to me, I actually saw that scene in my youth watching an Americanized anime called Star Blazers (Space Battleship Yamoto), EXACTLY that scene.  (Edit: and I just remembered another thing - he lifts, lock stock and barrel, the way space combat is conducted from Vinge's Fire Upon the Deep - he is very ballsy, considering he copied from a Hugo award winnner and Nebula nominee)    But the problem isn't merely that Alastair Reynolds uses other peoples' ideas, it's that he fumbles every opportunity to write scifi - borrowed or not.  An interesting concept is mentioned and he simply does not develop it or study its effect on humans.  For instance, his little \\"wrinkle\\" in Vinge's trading family concept was that they are clones and all share the same memory - and two of them are in a relationship together.  That's an interesting concept, one worth exploring - how would that change the interactions between all the clones at the reunion, how would that relationship work?  But it's just a by-the-by thing - the clones are said to be clones, but sameness and shared memories never really come up, there isn't anything interesting, unusual or different about how they deal with each other or their society.  Had Reynolds explored the concept at all, it might have been science fiction.  Instead, the opposite is true throughout the book - things that SHOULD make life different for people are there, but over and over again these things are of no consequence, they affect nothing.    ...So it's an adventure tale using mostly borrowed scifi concepts.  Having gotten its concepts from elsewhere, an adventure book could STILL be a cracking good read.  Sadly it was not.  A well-written story gives you at least somewhat vivid characterization... the two main characters are virtually interchangable and both barely discernable - not for lack of space or time to develop them, this is a thick book at 565 pages.  While the 2 main characters have that wishy-washy interchangeable Rozencranz & Guildenstern-like quality, the other characters are even more washed-out.  One is missing an eye - that's the only really distinguishing feature I can remember - but generally Reynolds' attempts at times of giving these other characters some character is to have them all take turns being sort of... bitchy, all of them in the same way.  Needless to say, this is not enough to establish characterization.  Characterization is a skill simply lacking in Alastair Reynold's toolkit, judging from this book.    As a mystery, it is fairly ham-handed.  I will restrain from going into the dreary details but if you want a mystery - or if you want a scifi mystery - you can do a lot better.  Having failed to be a science fiction novel, it isn't a rousing mystery either.  Many alternatives are available that will engage you, get you involved in the mystery, the characters, and so on.      If you're looking for space-opera-y scifi, try the guys he is stealing from:    Vernor Vinge  [[ASIN:0812536355 A Deepness in the Sky]]  [[ASIN:0812515285 A Fire Upon The Deep]]    Wil McCarthy  [[ASIN:055358443X The Collapsium]]  [[ASIN:0553584464 The Wellstone]]  [[ASIN:0553584472 Lost in Transmission]]  [[ASIN:055358717X To Crush the Moon]]    ...or if you want an engaging space-opera mystery/adventure that ranges all over, try James Alan Gardner  [[ASIN:038079439X Expendable (League of Peoples, Bk. 1)]]  [[ASIN:0380813297 Ascending]]  [[ASIN:0380802090 Hunted (League of Peoples, Bk. 4)]]  [[ASIN:B000BOB2ZW Radiant]]    Or if you want more of a sci-fi noir type mystery  Gun, with Occasional Music by Johnathan Lethem  -  ASIN:0156028972   Only Forward by Micheal Marshall Smith  -  ASIN:0553579703  (you can only put so many links in a review or I'd link these too)    Oh and I almost forgot to mention, the pivotal \\"thing\\" in the mystery/tale ends up being sentient humanoid robots.  This is a sort of old-fashioned concept - most scifi writers now seem to be aware of the concept of the tech singularity and now take it into account.  There's probably no way you would end up with anthropomorphic humanlike robot AI, but if you DID want to take that as a premise for a story, then you could go back to 1953 for Asimov's various robot mystery books starting with Caves of Steel.  Reynolds had 55 years of actual advancement - over half a century - since Asimov wrote that robot mystery scifi book, but couldn't think of any interesting, stimulating, or even entertaining ideas having to do with anthropomorphic robots.  He had no new concepts nor did he revisit the old ones.  So if you're looking for a book about intelligent robots involved in a scifi mystery, read Caves of Steel and its sequels - much better writing and ideas in that book.  Oddly, more seems to happen and the characters seem more vivid even though it is less than half the length of House of Suns - as are, I believe, all of the recommendations above.    Any of the books I've mentioned will be infinitely better than House of Suns by Alastair Reynolds.  I use my copy as kindling when I camp rather than selling it to a used bookstore - it would hurt me to know it was out there.

Their disdain did nothing to derail Dylan's intent and herein lies the album where he met a critical challenge. As a linguistics enthusiast he built the template and opened up the world of potential in songwriting, making it as viable as poetry. On musical grounds, the other great boast he can claim his own is the invention of Rock. It's here that he perfected his electric Chicago blues using rock aesthetic to fit into the mold of his wordplay. This realization would have been inconceivable without the contributions of lead guitarist and comrade Mike Bloomfield. Part of Dylan's genius is he knew instinctively that this was the man to commission.<br /><br />Never more in his career did Bob capture the tenor of the times than on \\"Like A Rolling Stone\\", the most intuitive anthem in rock that was completed in a one-day session. Each time the verses and the chorus of \\"How does it feel?\\" arrived, they unearthed the deepest sources of his indictment of elitism. Generations later, this anthem of all anthems remains as bracing and as relevant as ever. His cynicism surfaced again in \\"Ballad of A Thin Man\\" whose opaque references darted at a journalist were darn near impossible to decode. Because the banging piano chords were so apt in driving the contents of its message, it eventually clicked.<br /><br />The Dylan-Bloomfield axis attained its summit on the blues shuffle \\"It Takes A Lot to Laugh, It Takes A Train to Cry\\" and on two briskly-paced blues \\"From A Buick 6\\" and \\"Highway 61 Revisited\\". The frisson in Dylan's vocals on his literary surrealism and Bloomfield's blistering axe solos made these positively intoxicating.<br /><br />But it's the last track, \\"Desolation Row\\", that was so terminal that in no way could it have been slotted elsewhere. Arguably (or not), this could be the greatest song in his entire canon in which he easily headed that list of greatest songwriters. Dylan painted abstract moods of numerous characters in a sonic Wasteland that was enormous in its scope. T.S. The implication of increased stability in the product description and related reviews may indicate to some that there is some sort of passive or active stabilization effect introduced by the X-Grip.  There is nothing that adds stability in this device except that it sometimes may reduce the shake of an awkward position.  The X-Grip is a hard plastic C-shaped grip with a padded handle to which you attach your camera in variable places that may allow you to hold your camera at a different angle or position than what is normally your comfortable vertical or horizintal position.  If you are a photographer of some experience, you have already figured out how to grip your camera and place your body in ways that best capture your subject, even if it is uncomfortable for a period of time.  The X-Grip is a device that requires one to learn how to use changes in grip.  But, when you experiment with different positions afforded by the X-Grip, the shutter button and other controls on your camera which were designed to be ergonomic and within the reach of certain fingers when the camera is held in a normal fashion, may not be positioned in a practical place when the X-Grip is used.  So while I think the X-Grip may bring improved camera holding positions with respect to the lens and its relationship with the subject, those positions may make adjustments to the camera very difficult.  I have found that there are some positions that give a better perspective of the subject, but such framing takes additional time to adjust your camera because the controls are in an relatively unfamiliar location.  Overall, I have to rate the X-Grip with 3 stars because it is not an improvement in the process of all the pictures you take, and the time it may take to change your camera to the X-Grip mount could cause you to lose a great image. 0316098329|5.0|ROOM is an uncomfortable, heartbreaking, wonderful book.  It's one of those books that I picked up expecting one thing, but got something completely different.  I did not expect a five year old narrator throughout the entire story and, frankly, it took some time to get past the narrator's voice and sentence structure, etc., but once past the first twenty pages, the book simply flew by.  I literally could not put it down.    There were a few areas that drew me out of the book - the fact that a five year old manages to outwit an adult and escape into a world he never knew existed was one of those areas, but it needed to happen to move the story forward.  The other area that made me wonder was how easily Jack was sent to live with the grandparents while Ma was recovering - I'm not sure that would actually happen in real life, but the story didn't suffer because of it.    I think that the author did a terrific job of seeing the world through the eyes of a five year old captive. I think she also did a wonderful job of giving us insight into what Ma's life was like before and after with just a few brush strokes in her writing.  The biggest drawback to me for this book was the breastfeeding - I literally became queasy each time it was mentioned after the first two or three times, and I'm not sure if that was the intention of the author.  It was such a huge part of the book, so that would be the only negative (IMHO).    But overall, I think ROOM was a wonderful story told with tenderness and love and it shows how strong the bonds of love and family are, even in the most horrific of circumstances.  I would recommend this book to anyone.

0875428940|5.0|The Robin Wood Tarot is a more than a redressing of the Rider Tarot into the pagan tradition.  It's one of those classic decks that deserves at least a second look, and should not be avoided by tarot collectors.<br /><br />The back of these cards are decorated with a celtic knot work in white and green over a black background.  These cards are bordered white, most of the time numbered and labeled at the bottom.  The imagery in these cards is realistic, but a bit cartoony at times.  Also, beware that a few of these cards show nudity, but this is done tastefully.<br /><br />The Major Arcana are mostly numbered and labeled at the bottom, often within a scroll.  The imagery in these cards follows a more pagan tradition.  For example, usually in many pagan/celtic oriented tarots, the devil is replaced with chains.  This tarot is no exception, where the standard imagery has been replaced with two naked people pulling a chained treasure toward the end of a tunnel.  In a twist, the chains form an upside-down pentagram.<br /><br />The pips in the Minor Arcana are numbered at the bottom within a white circle.  These cards integrate the standard imagery of swords, wands, cups or pentacles, but include no label as none is needed.  The court cards follow the standard labels of knave, knight, queen, or king also at the bottom.<br /><br />I recommend for beginners that favor Pagan over Christian imagery; and without a doubt, this is a must have for tarot collectors.  These cards come in a box which opens at the side like a chest, and includes a little white book.

0674057929|5.0|This book contains the story 'The Picture of Dorian Gray' - in original typescript form.  This is the way Oscar Wilde wanted the novel to actually read before the publishers got hold of it.  There are two other versions of the novel out there - the version printed for magazine publication & the one printed in novel form.  After the publishers got hold of the manuscript a lot of changes were made because of content - the homoerotic element was toned down.  The novel version is also longer - Wilde expanded the story.  There are pictures, illustrations, & extensive notes included to explain all the changes.  It's very interesting to see a version in print that is how Wilde intended it.  If you are a fan of Oscar Wilde's works or of Dorian Gray in particular, you will appreciate having this book.

But there are still the moral questions of whether or not they should die for human consumption when we can fairly easily survive eating things other than meat. And of course the environmental impact of raising so many animals for the increasing human population. Of course I think about the question of how these animals are raised and if it is in complete misery then of course I think they shouldn't be alive at all - afterall, most everyone believes in humane euthanasia. After struggling with these questions among others I bought \\"Eating Animals\\" on a whim at an airport one day and I am so glad I did. Jonathan Foer intelligently discusses (from a lay person's point of view) the moral dilemmas that arise from eating meat and whether or not they should stop us from doing so. The book provided me with researched answers to the questions I had been wondering about myself for some time. I thought this book was great and highly recommend it to anyone who just doesn't really know on which side of the fence they should fall.
0393327345|4.0|Not the best book, but I say to put down the bookmark because this book is best read in one sitting.  It doesn't work quite as well if you have to read it in smaller chunks.
