0312600844|4.0|Open this book to the inside of the back flap; observe the author's photograph; notice the near chiaroscuro forming around the outlines of Frazen's head.  Yes; that is a halo you are detecting, and Frazen is a member of the chosen.  Or so people say.    Frazen hit the literary world's jackpot with The Corrections, and not only did people laud that novel, but Oprah gave it a most profitable edge of controversy.  Yeah, I mean what better way to take a novel that probably was not going to have a huge audience and reach millions than to snub the talkshow queen, America's beloved?  So of course the next big novel from Frazen, Freedom, was met with excitement and expectation.    Now, before I jump into the review here, I just want to address one little thing.  The author has some fairly vocal females making some pretty strong accusations about him and what his presence has meant for women writers.  I am not going to go into that on too deep of a level because it is neither here nor there, for now anyway.  But I do want to say this: children, remember there is enough room in this sandbox for us all to play together.    Synopsis    \\"It was the season of migration, of flight and song and sex.  Down in the neotropics, where diversity was as great as anywhere on earth, a few hundred bird species grew restless and left behind the several thousand other species, many of them close taxonomic relatives, that were content to stay put and crowdedly coexist and reproduce at their tropical leisure.\\" (pg. 485)      I chose this quote because I found that it sheds a little light on the theme of the book, while also cleverly using birds to create an allegory for the characters of the novel.  I hesitated to use this quote, though, because it is also a little misleading.  The book is not nearly as dry as the quote.      Let me explain.  First: theme.  The artwork on the cover clearly takes a stand that this book has something to do with birds, or wilderness maybe.  If you look closely at it, you'll notice the reflection of the woods in the lake is pixelated, so then we must also consider the human thumbprint on ecology.  Are you with me so far?  An exciting new genre has been taking shape lately, and that is Ecoliterature.   Okay, perhaps \\"new\\" isn't the right word (a-hem, sorry Walden) but certainly the genre is burgeoning.    It takes the novel a good llloooonnnnnggggg while to reach the point where you read about any birds.  So for maybe half the novel you'll find yourself thinking that the cover is just pretty artwork or that they chose a bird because birds can fly and therefore often represent freedom (a concept much easier to spot in the novel's earlier chapters).  But if you keep going, at some point you will meet the Cerulean warbler, a bird!  Walter Berglund - the patriarch of the Berglund family (who taken together are all protagonists of this novel) will at some point advocate for the protection of these birds.  And folks, you might want to read this part when you are wide awake and not distracted because the birds, for Walter, are only ever a symbol, a tool to use to get at his real preoccupation: overpopulation.  So there you go, the cover's artwork and Ecoliterature.    Now remember how I said that the quote about these warblers is also an allegory for the Berglund family?  Well, let me unpack that a little.  On the surface, this novel is primarily about various members of the Berglund family in various stages of their lives: starting with young yuppie gentrification and ending in pseudo exile.  These people migrate, sing, fly.  And oh yes, they are restless, just like the quote says!  Each chapter will address a different Berglund, the mistakes he/she is busy making or repenting, and all the while we will march relentlessly forward in time as this novel spans decades.  By and large, the Berglunds are flawed; this works nicely because their flaws are what makes them real for readers.  And in some ways this book is meant as a warning, aforeshadowing of things to come and what has already begun, ecologically speaking.  So it helps us readers take the warning seriously if we can relate to the ugly zits popping up on the tender fresh Berglund skin.    Scoring  (Click here to understand my rating methodology)  Readability: I find Frazen's prose to be rock solid.  However that rock can sometimes be unyielding.  Don't get me wrong, this stuff is good - but I am not dying to read more.  And it doesn't help that he writes such long books because by the end any urge to move forward is simply an urge to be done.  Hey - I'm a slow reader!     1 out of 2 pts.      Freshness: Ahm not so much no.  Good book but I don't see that it really breaks with conventions in any significant way.  I suppose that some might be arguing that having the protagonist(s) be antihero(s) might be new, but it isn't.  I suppose some might be arguing that having the story told through multiple viewpoints is new, but it isn't.  It is called postmodernism people!  Maybe some think the \\"autobiography\\" section was fresh - I don't know, I'll admit it was creative but the idea doesn't seem significant enough to me to reverberate into an actual point on my scoring system.  And though the book is part of an emerging genre, I am just not satisfied with it enough to fulfill this particular criteria.  0 out of 2 pts.     Author-Exhibits-Writerly-Traits-Which-Impress-Me: Oh yes, I am stunned by the amount of planning that was put into this book.  And I found the hunt for the title and subsequent dizzying tally of all the different meanings/usages of the word to be too much for me to keep up with.  So much so that I thought a) this would be a good topic for an academic paper; then immediately thought b) thank god I don't have to do that work!  Also there was some very enlightening historical analysis happening in there that I was totally benighted to before the reading of this book.  3*** out of 2 pts.     Pathos: Yeah, there is some there; not aton, but some.  See here is my problem with the antiheroic protagonist: I am just never going to love him/her.  If they struggle to love themselves then as a reader I have to too.  I liked the characters the best at the outset, and with each turning of the page loved them less.  Sparks is heartless, what can I say? 1 out of 2 pts.     Je Ne Sais Quoi: There was something that felt good about this book.  A little bit of swagger from the author perhaps.  At any rate, I felt like I was in good hands.  It is always a plus when the novel you read you suspect might raise your IQ rather than lower it, even if only marginally.  2 out of 2 pts.     7 out of 10 points.  So you see I do not love this book nearly as much as many other reviewers do, but I still back it as time well spent!  Like that cover too! 0393327655|5.0|It is ironic that Sam Harris begins his book with a description of a young man bombing a bus in the heart of a city.  Though published in 2004, the description appears to fit very well the recent London transit bombings.  And this is precisely why this book demands our attention in this time of growing radical, religious fundamentalism of whatever variety.<br /><br />Sam Harris presents as a quiet, thoughtful, reflective person in television interviews and public presentations.  His background is in philosophy and he is now completing his doctorate in neuro-science.  He presents his analysis of religion in a deliberate, careful, rational manner.  Yet the result is powerful.<br /><br />The book his two major themes.  The first part is comprised of a critique of the irrational basis of religious faith and the often terrible consequences of these beliefs.<br /><br />This is not a tentative or hesitating criticism.  At a time when the negative effects of religion and religious thinking are becoming increasingly visible, this book serves notice that making accommodations to religious thinking serves only to allow it to perpetuate its destructive influence.  A belief that killing innocent people is responding to the will of one's God is not an idea to be given credence.  And yet it flows directly from religious ideology and scripture.<br /><br />After surveying the current effects of religious beliefs, Harris then explores the nature of belief and how it relates to reason by providing an excellent review of the criteria and process of determining truth--or what in philosophy is called epistemology.<br /><br />Building on this analysis, he then reviews the effect of irrational belief in the history of Christianity with the Inquisition, the Cathar persecution, the witch hunts and finally the Holocaust.  His point is that the moderation and toleration that is generally accepted today is not a result of the religious belief itself, but the modulating influence of the Enlightenment and the political separation of church and state that followed.  This is followed by a detailed chapter analyzing the rise of radical and violent Islam.  But lest we think we are immune from the effect of religious fundamentalism, he points out its current effect over issues such as the Ten Commandments controversy, the role of \\"faith-based\\" legislative efforts, the attempt to legislate what had previously been areas of private freedom, the movement to control embryonic stem cell research,  and, of course, the abortion debate.<br /><br />Harris is particularly critical of what he calls the \\"myth of moderation\\" which flows from the postmodern viewpoint that all ideas are relative and none can be held truer or better than others.<br /><br />Moderates do not want to kill anyone in the name of God, but they want us to keep using the word \\"God\\" as though we know what we were talking about.  And they do not want anything too critical said about people who really believe in the God of their fathers, because tolerance, perhaps above all else, is sacred.  To speak plainly and truthfully about the state of the world--to say, for instance, that the Bible and Koran both contain mountains of life-destroying gibberish--is antithetical to tolerance as moderates currently conceive it.  But we can no longer afford the luxury of such political correctness.  We must finally recognize the price we are paying to maintain the iconography of our ignorance.<br /><br />The second part of the book is what makes it so significant  This is not just another attack on the irrationality of religious faith.  Harris acknowledges the legitimacy of the issues that religion attempts to address.<br /><br />What makes one person happier than another?  Why is love more conducive to happiness than hate?  Why do we generally prefer beauty to ugliness and order to chaos?  Why does it feel so good to smile and laugh, and why do these shared experiences generally bring people closer together?  Is the ego and illusion, and, if so, what implications does this have for human life?  Is there life after death?  These are ultimately questions for a mature science of the mind.  If we ever develop such a science, most of our religious  texts will be no more useful to mystics than they are now to astronomers.<br /><br />First he addresses ethics.  What kind of ethics is possible without a faith in a supernatural God?  One based in reason and that incorporates our growing knowledge of ourselves at the level of the brain.  Where currently there is little consensus on moral issues, a sustained inquiry will force the convergence  of various belief systems as it has done in other sciences.  Moral relativism will no  longer make sense (\\"we can't really judge the suicide bomber\\") because we will have developed verifiable criteria for moral and ethical behavior.  Harris explores a number of contemporary issues from this perspective including terrorism, torture and pacifism.  Furthermore, ethics is intimately connected with spirituality.<br /><br />In the next chapter he reframes the entire arena of spirituality from the religious to the scientific in the newly emerging field of consciousness studies.  He is hesitant to use the words spirituality or mysticism because \\"neither word captures the reasonableness and profundity of the possibility that we must now consider: that there is a form of well-being that supersedes all others, indeed, that transcends the vagaries of experience itself\\".  Specifically he refers to those traditions that identify spirituality with consciousness itself--with  the observer of content rather than the content itself, which frees us from the vicissitudes of experience.<br /><br />Our spiritual traditions suggest that we have considerable room here to change our relationship to the contents of consciousness, and thereby to transform our experience of the world.  Indeed, a vast literature on human spirituality attests to this.  It is also clear that nothing need be believed on insufficient evidence for us to look in this possibility with an open mind.<br /><br />It is tempting to quote whole sections of this final chapter in which Harris rescues spirituality from religion.  He explores the nature of consciousness and the various efforts within traditional religions to change the nature of consciousness through sustained introspection and the refinement of attention.  He applies this to an analysis of the nature of the self--how it arises, what sustains it and how it can be transcended.  He compares Eastern to Western philosophy and religion and questions why the Eastern analysis appears to be so much more sophisticated.  And finally he describes meditation as a form of introspection in a section which can serve as a primer to meditative practice.  All of this is done from an empirical perspective informed by modern studies of consciousness rather than from religious doctrine.<br /><br />The only lack in this book is the omission of the psychodynamic explanation for faith as originally proposed by Freud and more recently in the book The Psychological Roots of Religious Belief by M.D. Faber.  Harris takes a more cultural and societal  perspective.<br /><br />Few books describe more clearly the transition to a post-religious era and establish  so clearly why it is of such importance.<br /><br />The days of our religious identities are clearly numbered.  Whether the days of our civilization itself are numbered would seem to depend, rather too much, on how soon we realize this.

0062060554|4.0|Few debut novels can  provide both good reading AND good writing.  Yet S.J. Watson accomplishes just that with his psychological thriller Before I Go To Sleep.  Although I can give it only 4 stars, I strongly recommend this book.<br /><br />GOOD READING:<br /><br />The main thrust was really intriguing for me and had me asking questions before I even read the first page.  How can Christine - a woman with severe memory damage (her amnesia recurs every morning when she wakes up) - stay safe in her world, let alone thrive and live anything akin to a normal life?  How does she address marriage, friendship, her home, even her own reflection in the mirror when she cannot recognize the husband she finds herself sleeping next to?  How can she gain enough traction each day to move forward with her life without being completely debilitated by her condition?<br /><br />Finally, how does an author add the element of mystery to such a scenario?  I found Watson's answers to all this to be very believable and keenly entertaining.<br /><br />GOOD WRITING:<br /><br />Watson's style is easy to read, straightforward, and his writing deftly overcomes three key challenges created by his potentially restrictive plot construction:<br /><br />1)  The repetition inherent in a tale of amnesia:  Watson manages to make each day seem different enough to keep the reader turning the pages.  He spares us having to endure too much rehashing of Christine's daily reawakening and re-remembering.  I was amazed at how much new information Watson was able to parse into each of Christine's days.<br /><br />2) The linearity of a diary approach:  Using flashbacks, new observations, and the occasional nuggets of curiously inconsistent details, Watson is able to give the story full depth and intrigue.  Instantly, I became captivated with each page and each day, as subtle changes cause Christine to doubt the trustworthiness of everyone around her.  In fact, there are points at which the reader is drawn into this web by questioning even Christine's own memories, motives, and integrity. Remarkably, Watson stays quite tightly to a sequential chronology.<br /><br />3) The singular nature of a purely first-person narrative:  Cleverly, Watson keeps us in Christine's head without allowing us to get bored.  We get a strong sense of the world around her, and what could have been a very narrow perspective is widened to engaging effect.<br /><br />MINOR DISAPPOINTMENTS:<br /><br />My own negatives with this book are few, but worth mentioning as they are the reasons I could not give  5 stars:<br /><br />1) The ultimate conceit of the novel will be easy to unveil by many readers who have seen enough movies.  To his credit, Watson manages to build sufficient dread to carry any correct-guessers through to the final pages.  (Even though I was pretty sure I knew what was happening, I was compelled to keep reading.)<br /><br />2) The ending to the ending is a bit hurried and lackluster.<br /><br />3) The F-bombs.  Although there are not a lot of them, I found them jarring, completely unnecessary, and artificially added as if for special effect.  I personally and generally do not mind such language, but in this case it pops up abruptly and pointlessly, and not at all in keeping with the characters.<br /><br />BOTTOM LINE:<br /><br />This is a skillfully architected read and not as deeply convoluted as other memory genre works which come to my mind, such as the movies The Jacket, The Butterfly Effect, or (my favorite) Alejandro Amenabar's Open Your Eyes.  In fact, it struck me as a more harrowing psycho-dramatic version of the comedy 50 First Dates,  where Drew Barrymore's character has to watch a video tape every day to remind her about her current life.  But in the case of Before I Go To Sleep, that \\"video\\" is corruptible and might not exist for her own best interests.<br /><br />Watson is most definitely on my watch list.emories, motives, and integrity. Remarkably, Watson stays quite tightly to a sequential chronology.    3) The singular nature of a purely first-person narrative:  Cleverly, Watson keeps us in Christine's head without allowing us to get bored.  We get a strong sense of the world around her, and what could have been a very narrow perspective is widened to engaging effect.     MINOR DISAPPOINTMENTS:    My own negatives with this book are few, but worth mentioning as they are the reasons I could not give  5 stars:    1) The ultimate conceit of the novel will be easy to unveil by many readers who have seen enough movies.  To his credit, Watson manages to build sufficient dread to carry any correct-guessers through to the final pages.  (Even though I was pretty sure I knew what was happening, I was compelled to keep reading.)     2) The ending to the ending is a bit hurried and lackluster.     3) The F-bombs.  Although there are not a lot of them, I found them jarring, completely unnecessary, and artificially added as if for special effect.  I personally and generally do not mind such language, but in this case it pops up abruptly and pointlessly, and not at all in keeping with the characters.     BOTTOM LINE:     This is a skillfully architected read and not as deeply convoluted as other memory genre works which come to my mind, such as the movies The Jacket, The Butterfly Effect, or (my favorite) Alejandro Amenabar's Open Your Eyes.   In fact, it struck me as a more harrowing psycho-dramatic version of the comedy 50 First Dates,  where Drew Barrymore's character has to watch a video tape every day to remind her about her current life.  But in the case of Before I Go To Sleep, that \\"video\\" is corruptible and might not exist for her own best interests.      Watson is most definitely on my watch list.
